#### Using Azure SQL
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=0)** - As SQL Database and SQL Server have evolved over the years, so have their deployment options.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=7)** With Azure SQL Database, the SQL databases and SQL server instances you know can now be hosted and managed completely in the cloud.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=16)** By going cloud first on your databases, you get the same benefits as any other cloud-first resource you use, like easy scaling, global availability, or hands-off administration.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=27)** There are two primary ways Azure SQL can be used to fit your data needs.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=32)** Each determine how much control you have over the infrastructure resources and the cost.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=38)** The first option is through hosted infrastructure.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=41)** Here SQL Server runs on Azure VMs.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=45)** With this approach you have greater control over the data base engine, since you have access to the infrastructure it runs on.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=52)** This option is great if you need the ability to fully customize your SQL server instance.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=58)** It's also great for existing applications that require a quick migration to the cloud with barely any changes.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=65)** And it's the best option if you need custom development or test environments and don't want to buy additional on-premise hardware, which can get real expensive.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=76)** The alternative options are to use Azure SQL databases or managed SQL instances.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=82)** With these options, you give all the database maintenance tasks, like applying patches or upgrading SQL Server versions, to Azure instead of doing them yourself.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=91)** You also get to use the latest stable SQL Server features, as the hardware and software you use will be fully managed, owned, and hosted by Microsoft.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=101)** For Azure SQL databases, there are three deployment options to choose from.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=106)** First, there's the single database, which has its own set of resources managed by a database server.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=112)** Single-database deployments are built for cloud-native applications and even have hyperscale and serverless tiers.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=120)** Hyperscale is a special service tier in Azure SQL Database that basically removes any of the practical limits set on normal cloud databases.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=129)** With hyperscale database deployments, a hundred terabyte databases, minute-long restores, and almost instantaneous backups are all possible.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=140)** Serverless, within the context of Azure SQL Database, is a compute tier that's build for serverless applications.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=147)** It scales compute resources based on workload demand and even pauses the database when it's not active, just like serverless apps do.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=156)** Second, there's the elastic pool, which is a collection of databases with a shared set of resources.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=163)** This is also managed by a database server.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=166)** Elastic pool deployments provide a more cost-effective way to deal with multiple databases that have different usage patterns.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=174)** This includes single-database types.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=177)** And finally, there's the database server itself, which is used to manage the groups of single databases and elastic pools you may have.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/using-azure-sql?u=76281980&t=187)** As you start thinking about potential database migrations to the cloud, or creating new ones, check out the Feature Comparison page for Azure SQL Database to see the full list of differences and to find the ones that matter to you.

### 6. 5. Azure Functions

#### What is an Azure Function?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - While developing applications, there will be times where we just need to perform some sort of action.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=6)** Maybe we need to copy a file for backup purposes, or we need to kickstart a web job based on some sort of state.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=13)** Azure functions work well for these scenarios.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=16)** With Azure functions, we only need to write the code needed to perform the action we want without requiring the full blown infrastructure that larger applications may need.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=27)** At a minimum, Azure functions will always have two important parts.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=31)** The code itself, and some sort of configuration file.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=35)** Usually denoted as a function.json file.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=39)** If you use a scripting language to write your code with, say, Powershell, you'll need to create this configuration file yourself.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=47)** Otherwise, if using compiled languages like C Sharp or Java, this config file will be automatically generated for you from the annotations in your code.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=57)** This config file holds several key pieces of information for your function.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=62)** The most important of which is the bindings property.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=66)** It's here that you'll set the binding type, the direction your function data flows, and the name of the bound data.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=74)** It could also have more information depending on the binding type you choose.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=79)** With just these two parts, your functions are ready to use.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=82)** Say your application deals with many files uploads and each requires some scrubbing before proceeding.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=87)** You could create a function that uses a blob trigger to do that, before adding it to an Azure blob storage container.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=94)** How about using HTTP triggers to generate a coupon for a customer once they have finished their purchase?
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=100)** A function is a great use case for this smaller piece of functionality.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=104)** There are many other types of triggers that Azure functions use, but we'll talk about them a bit later.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=110)** Once you've created a function, you deploy them for use via a function app.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=115)** Function apps can house multiple functions and is great for organizing related functions into a single, manageable, and logical unit.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=125)** If you're thinking about using Azure functions, it's recommended to start with runtime version two, as most languages are supported and are in a stable state.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=135)** Version three is available, but it's currently in preview, so it's best only if you want to experiment with Azure Functions that support .NET Core 3.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=144)** As of this recording, version three is not recommended for use in production yet.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=150)** When creating a function app, however, you won't have to worry, as it is set to version two automatically.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=155)** It's also important to note that starting with version two of Azure Functions runtime, all functions in the same function app must be written in the same language.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=165)** Before, the model allowed functions to be written in different languages.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=169)** This is also good to keep in mind if you're migrating from a previous version to version two or higher.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=176)** Azure Functions has two pricing models, and depending on how you use functions, one or the other may be better for you.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=183)** There's the consumption plan, which only charges you based on the compute resources you use, meaning you only pay when your functions are running.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=190)** With this plan, Azure automatically and dynamically scales instances of your function hosts as needed, even during periods of high load.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=199)** This can be a great option if you have fluctuating workloads or can't otherwise comfortably predict expected usage.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=207)** If your functions need a custom image to run or you already have existing VMs that are running other app services, then choosing the app service plan may be better for you.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=217)** This allows you to manage your functions just like your other web apps.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=221)** If you can't afford to wait for potential cold start delays, aka additional time spent waiting for Azure to wake up and kick start an instance, you can use the always on setting that is only available through the app service plan.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=236)** To get started with Azure functions, you can download the Azure functions extension for VSCode, Azure functions core tools for use with the command prompt or terminal, or the Azure development workload for Visual Studio 2019.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-an-azure-function?u=76281980&t=250)** If you want to get coding right now, you can also head to the Azure portal and start coding your first function there.

>

>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-function-with-a-blobtrigger?u=76281980&t=275)** Bindings are an extremely useful feature in Azure functions, as they allow us to write much simpler code.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-function-with-a-blobtrigger?u=76281980&t=282)** First, we'll use the in binding to tell our function which source file to use.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-function-with-a-blobtrigger?u=76281980&t=288)** We denote this by using the blob trigger type followed by the path that we are expecting this file.
>

>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-function-with-a-blobtrigger?u=76281980&t=303)** We start with a blob type, followed by the path of the backup file's destination.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-function-with-a-blobtrigger?u=76281980&t=308)** We also explicitly declare write access using a trigger attribute.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-function-with-a-blobtrigger?u=76281980&t=314)** This will also be a stream type parameter.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-function-with-a-blobtrigger?u=76281980&t=317)** Finally, we await a copy function using the stream's CopyToAsync method.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-function-with-a-blobtrigger?u=76281980&t=323)** I'll even add some additional logs for a thorough history.
>

#### Other function trigger types
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=0)** - All Azure Functions require exactly one trigger, which is the action that starts the execution of your function code.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=8)** There are a variety of trigger types available that work with many common scenarios.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=12)** For example, TimerTriggers are great for executing functions on a predetermined schedule that you define.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=18)** Use TimerTriggers to say, retrigger builds on Netlifly to publish scheduled blog posts, or to kick off a daily web job to process help request tickets.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=27)** Another example are QueueTriggers, which allow you to respond to messages as they arrive in an Azure storage queue.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=35)** These can be essential to functions that process a lot of data, like resolving matches for a catalog of products, or logging some metadata before moving the message to the next phase of a workflow.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=46)** If a queue trigger happens to fail, Azure Functions automatically retries the function up to five more times before adding it to something called a poison queue.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=56)** With this separation, you can even write another function to handle failed messages, giving you flexibility on how to handle these exceptions.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=65)** CosmosDBTriggers are another trigger type available and are exactly what they sound like.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=71)** They kickstart functions whenever additions or updates occur in a no SQL database.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=77)** EvenGridTriggers respond to events delivered to a subscription in Azure Event Grid, an eventing service.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=84)** You can subscribe to a multitude of events and kickstart your function based on those subscription's new notifications.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=91)** By subscribing to a blog created event, for example, you can kickstart an image resizing function every time a new photo is uploaded to your Blob Storage.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=101)** You can even customize your Event Grid subscription with subject filtering.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=105)** That way, only events that match your filters execute your resizing function.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=111)** EventHugTriggers respond to events delivered to Azure Event Hub, a big data streaming platform and event ingestion service.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=120)** This is better suited for workflows working with IoT and application instrumentation.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=126)** Azure Event Hub in particular has one distinct characteristic over Azure Event Grid, and that's ordering.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=134)** Azure Event Hub processes events in order, which can be a good thing and a bad thing.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=141)** If your process relies on a specific order like requiring specific approvals from specific people, then choosing Azure Event Hub to process those events along with EventHubTriggers to help you track each step may be the better solution for you.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=157)** Just as there is support for normal storage queues via queue triggers, there's also support for service bus queues via ServiceBusQueueTriggers.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=166)** Choosing between storage queues and service bus queues, which determines which triggers you should use, is highly dependent on what your application's needs are, so I won't go into too much detail about them here.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=179)** You can check this link though to view a comparison of each solution and what considerations to keep in mind while choosing one.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/other-function-trigger-types?u=76281980&t=187)** With so many trigger types, I'm sure you'll find the perfect ones to use with your Azure Functions.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** function (6), require (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2)
> **CLI Commands:** find (1)
> **Env Vars:** sql (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)
> **Speakers:** - all (1)

#### When to use Durable Functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - When choosing between regular functions or durable functions.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=4)** One of the key deciding factors is whether or not you're implementing a sequential, multi-step, workflow, as part of your application.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=13)** If you have functionality in your app that depends on previous steps or specific states to trigger the next steps, durable functions make several tricky parts about these workflows much easier.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=26)** Durable functions are an extension of Azure functions that allow you to write stateful functions in a server less compute environment.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=35)** These are incredibly powerful when you have complex workflows or longer running dependent steps, and are the preferred solution over regular functions.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=45)** At its core, durable functions provides a library of useful obstructions and tools that simplify workflow orchestration.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=53)** This makes it easier to implement and define both workflow state and internal communication between functions.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=60)** Which means they are also much easier to understand.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=63)** Orchestration can occur with regular functions, but at the price of more overhead and a messier architecture.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=71)** Durable functions give us a cleaner architecture by their very composition.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=76)** All durable functions contain activity functions, and an orchestrator.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=81)** Activity Functions are essentially the same thing as Azure functions.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=84)** The difference here is that these activity functions can only be initiated by an activity trigger.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=91)** The orchestrator, also known as an orchestrator function, manages the flow of execution and data of any associated activity functions.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=102)** In doing so, it binds these activity functions into a single sequential workflow.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=108)** Let's say we had an extremely basic workflow that handles orders at a bakery.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=113)** This would start with charging a customer once they submit their online order, followed by an order request being generated for the pastry chefs to use and finally a text message to the customer once their order is ready.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=126)** Even with only three steps, this workflow has a moderate amount of complexity within it.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=132)** including a possible human interaction to trigger the final step.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=136)** With workflows like these, durable functions allow us to easily implement an orchestrator pattern.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=142)** But, without having to manage the messaging between functions ourselves, or worrying about workflow state the way we would if we implemented it in regular functions.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=151)** Creating workflows using durable functions also gives you a consolidated view of a workflow because the orchestration of all the steps will be in a single place, the orchestrator function.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=162)** And as an added bonus, this makes versioning your durable functions possible.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=167)** Using durable functions with workflows also allows you to check the status of your workflow via REST API.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=173)** Something that is a pain to do with a regular functions.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=177)** This is extremely useful to you as the developer, but, you could also extend this capability to provide a customer facing tracker.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=184)** so that your customers gain insight into the status of their order.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=188)** Having this REST API, also means that canceling a workflow is actually straightforward and seamless.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=195)** Something not so easy to do with regular functions.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=199)** These are just some of the key benefits of using durable functions, with fairly straightforward workflows.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=205)** As your workflows become more complex, you actually gain more benefits.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=209)** What if we took our previous workflow and added more steps, like a quick photo snap of the order before sending the text to the customer or an assignment task to a specific Baker?
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=221)** What if we also added a few parallel steps?
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=224)** Maybe generating an invoice when the text to the customer is sent, posting the photo to the bakery's Instagram account and decreasing stock from their inventory management system.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=235)** With durable functions, running multiple steps at once and keeping track of those steps is much easier to do.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=242)** Especially if we implemented this workflow with a fan-out fan-in pattern.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=247)** The fan-in part would be far more complex to implement with regular functions.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=252)** Without risk conditions occurring.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/when-to-use-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=254)** If you have any existing workflows, and I've encountered any of these obstacles, or, if you're researching the best solution for one you're about to implement, try creating a durable function and see how much easier it can be to implement maintainable workflows in code.

#### A closer look at Durable Functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's look at an example of a durable function in action.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=5)** I travel a lot to speak at conferences and found that I go through a similar set of preparations for each one.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=11)** I know that I have to book a flight, then book a hotel, and finally make a reservation at one the top-rated restaurants, yes it is a requirement.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=21)** In essence, this easily translates to a workflow that I can implement with a durable function.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=27)** Booking a flight, booking a hotel, and then making a reservation would all make perfect activity functions.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=35)** An orchestrator function can manage this workflow and ensures my activity functions are performed in their proper order.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=42)** If you're thinking, "But Adrienne, "this look like any other function "that uses a task-based API."
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=48)** It does but the implementation is actually quite different.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=52)** Let's take a closer look.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=54)** When the orchestrator makes the first invocation to book my flight, a queue message is actually sent from the orchestrator to the activity function.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=62)** In turn, the activity function gets triggered by the queue message, hopefully completes booking the ticket, serializes the result and sends it back as another queue message to the orchestrator.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=74)** When this message is received by the orchestrator, it triggers the second activity but with a clear difference.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=81)** It actually restarts from the beginning, from the first line of the function.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=86)** Since this booked ticket result is now assigned to the first variable, the orchestrator knows not to execute the booked ticket activity function again, and moves to the book hotel activity function instead.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=98)** With this understanding of how orchestrators work in durable functions, it's important to keep a few things in mind.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=105)** Because orchestrators will restart the execution flow each time it gets triggered, it is extremely important to write them in a deterministic way.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=114)** This means ensuring that no matter how many times the orchestrator runs, its output should always be the same.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=122)** So, using DateTime.Now, generating random GUIDs, or using multithread operations would not be advised as these do not result in a deterministic outputs.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=134)** The durable task framework that durable functions uses will do its best to detect possible violations in your code that would affect this replayability in orchestrators.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=144)** When it does find something, you'll receive a non-deterministic orchestration exception, but this obviously won't catch everything.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/a-closer-look-at-durable-functions?u=76281980&t=153)** So it's important to adhere to this deterministic constraint.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** function (9), let (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2), find (1)
> **Env Vars:** api (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - let (1)


### 7. 6. Virtual Machines and Batch

>

>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=99)** All that's left is to click the "review and create" button, and Azure will provision your VM.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=104)** If you prefer the command line or want to get up and running fast, then the Azure Cloud Shell is another option for provisioning a VM.

>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=128)** If you don't have a storage account connected, Azure will ask you to create a new one.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=132)** Note that this does incur a small cost.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=136)** Once you are connect to the Azure Cloud Shell, you'll need a total of two commands.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=141)** Really, that's it.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=142)** First, use the "az" group create command along with the name and location parameters.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=150)** Once your resource group is created, use the "az VM" create command followed by the resource group name, image, admin username, and admin password parameters.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=162)** Within a few minutes, your VM will be provisioned along with any resources it depends on.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=168)** You'll know the process was successful when you see this output.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=172)** Copy the public IP address value as you'll need that in the following step.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=177)** At this point, only RGP connections are opened on the VM you just created.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=183)** This is the default when creating a Windows VM in Azure.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=187)** To open it for web traffic, run the "az VM" open port command with the port, resource group, and name parameters.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=196)** Make sure to specify the port as "80", and to use your intended resource group and VM name.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=203)** That's it!
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/provision-a-virtual-machine?u=76281980&t=203)** You just provisioned a VM via the Azure Cloud Shell.

#### What is Azure Batch?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=0)** - Azure Batch is a managed service that allows you to run high-performance parallel computing jobs in the cloud.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=7)** In other words, if you need to process a lot of data within a relatively short amount of time, you'll probably use Azure Batch.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=16)** Instagram and Google are great examples of the sheer volume of data Azure Batch was built to handle.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=22)** During every minute of every single day, Instagram users upload around 49,000 photos and 3.8 million Google searches are performed.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=31)** To analyze all of this data in a timely, efficient, and reliable manner, it would take multiple compute cores deployed in clusters or grids all working together and auto-scaling as necessary to meet this kind of demand.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=47)** And, that's exactly what Azure Batch provides.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=50)** When you run a high-compute job with Azure Batch, it starts a pool of compute virtual machines for you, runs jobs to execute the tasks you have, while automatically re-queuing any failures, and then scales down the pool as work completes.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=65)** What's even better us that you control how quickly to complete these jobs and what your upper limit is on scaling.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=73)** This means you can control your costs while still meeting your deadlines.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=78)** Using Azure Batch to develop a large-scale parallel compute solution is pretty straightforward and follows a standardized process.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=86)** Let's say you need to process a cast amount of funny cat videos by compressing them before publishing to a public website, and since, you're a fairly popular site, because who doesn't love funny cat videos, you're averaging about 2000 files an hour that need to be processed.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=103)** How do you use Azure Batch?
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=105)** In this scenario, you'd first create an Azure Storage Account.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=109)** All cat videos would be uploaded here, and it becomes the input source for your batch service.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=116)** Any application files, like binaries or scripts, should also be uploaded into this storage account.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=122)** These are what Azure Batch's tasks will execute on the videos.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=126)** In our case, a simple compression script will do to downsize our videos.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=131)** Creating a pool of compute nodes, aka, the VMs that will execute your tasks, comes next.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=136)** Here, you decide how powerful your job can be.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=141)** Using the Azure CLI or the Azure portal, you'd specify the number of compute nodes for the pool, their individual size, and what operating system to use.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=151)** When your tasks run, it will be assigned to one of these nodes.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=155)** After the pool comes the actual job.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=158)** Each job is assigned a pool and manages all the tasks that are part of it.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=163)** Just like the pool of nodes, you'd create the job through the Azure CLI or Azure portal.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=169)** Finally, you add tasks to the job you just created.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=173)** These are the actual components that will run our compression script and will output the smaller videos to Azure Storage.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=180)** That's it.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=181)** Once your job has started you can monitor the job progress through the portal and retrieve your compressed cat videos from your Azure Storage account.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=189)** Or, if you really want to go above and beyond, you can automate the last step of publishing using a function and blog trigger.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=198)** This is just one example of how Azure Batch truly shines when it comes to high-compute processing, but its power isn't limited to processing cat videos.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=208)** Finance applications use Azure Batch to run financial risk simulations.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=213)** Animation and game studios take advantage of fast visual effects and 3D image rendering capabilities.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=220)** And geneticists harness its compute power for genetic sequence analysis.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=225)** And, for my fellow developers who want to make friends with their quality assurance teams, Azure Batch could also be used for software test execution.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=234)** Especially on large platforms with even larger test suites, offloading that amount of work to Azure Batch can probably cut your test execution times in a real good way.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=246)** As you'll be dealing a lot with infrastructure concepts, I highly recommend you to go over the Azure Batch best practices page.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/what-is-azure-batch?u=76281980&t=253)** And once you've mastered nodes, pools, and tasks, try creating your first batch job using the Azure CLI.

### 8. 7. Kubernetes and Containers in Azure

#### Container and Kubernetes Basics
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=0)** - Many applications are moving towards a container-based approach, and with good reason.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=5)** Containers allow us to separate our application's environmental concerns from the application itself.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=12)** Configuring our application to run on different target environments is time consuming.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=18)** With containers, we can explicitly state what kind of environment our application needs, and then have that environment automatically created for us instead.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=27)** If it's our local dev environment, our company's private data center, or a public cloud offering like Azure, containers give us the freedom not to care.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=37)** Since the exact same environment is created to host our application, it will probably work everywhere and not just on my machine.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=46)** To achieve this consistency, the explicit instructions that describe what our application needs and how to form its required environment are saved in a file known as the container image.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=59)** You can think of the container image as a recipe for a cake and the container itself as the actual cake.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=68)** I will get to you later.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=71)** In the same way that I can bake as many cakes as I'd like with a given recipe, you can create as many instances of a container as you'd like using a given container image.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=83)** Mm, two cakes are better than one.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=87)** Container images also work extremely well with a developer workflow we already know and love.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=94)** Just as our application source code is version-controlled, kept pristine, or hey, at least we try to, and shared in a repository, the container image can also be version-controlled and distributed anywhere from a repository of its own, the container registry.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=111)** After hearing all this, you may be asking, how are containers different from virtual machines?
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=117)** While some major concepts are similar between the two, like the ability to isolate resources or allocate specific ones, containers offer more benefits through its implementation.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=130)** Where virtual machines virtualize the hardware, containers virtualize the operating system instead.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=137)** This makes containers much more lightweight than virtual machines, and faster, as they only need to boot a fraction of the memory required rather than the entire operating system.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=149)** The most common container image specification used is Docker.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=152)** And many would consider Docker containers to be the industry standard.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=157)** Docker supports Linux and Windows environments, is open source, and is supported by all major cloud platforms, including Azure.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=166)** As are, if you work with Kubernetes and containers, you'll probably work with Docker.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=170)** If you're lucky and only have to deal with a single container, then running that single container is easy.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=177)** But for many companies, migrating to or focused on a container-based approach for their applications, the reality is that hundreds or thousands of containers are deployed at a time.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=189)** The total number is based on customer demand, supported versions, available budget for resources, and other variable things.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=198)** No matter what creates that volume, there's still an overwhelming amount of containers to manage.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=204)** This is where Kubernetes comes in.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=206)** Instead of having a single person or team manage the tedious tasks of deploying and scaling containerized applications, Kubernetes is given those responsibilities instead.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=219)** Stepping in as a container orchestration platform, Kubernetes automates these tasks based on constraints you choose.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=227)** These are things like resource consumption, load balancing limits, and availability limits.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=233)** Mix and match these constraints to your team's needs, then Kubernetes will handle the rest.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/container-and-kubernetes-basics?u=76281980&t=238)** Really, depending on the available resources it has access to, Kubernetes can automatically load balance requests across different instances of an application, move an application from one host to another if it detects a resource shortage or outage, and makes canary deployments a much, much simpler process.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** docker (4)
> **Code Keywords:** private (1), public (1), this, (1)
> **Documentation:** specification (1)
> **Exercise Files:** source code (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (1)
> **Speakers:** - many (1)

#### Create a managed Kubernetes cluster

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=0)** - Creating an Azure managed Kubernetes cluster is super simple.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=4)** Let's create one now using the Azure Cloud Shell.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=7)** As you can see, I'm already logged into my Azure account and I'm on the Azure portal.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=12)** Click on the Azure Cloud Shell icon in the top menu bar to start the Azure Cloud Shell.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=19)** You can maximize the window so that you can see more of your code.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=23)** Next, type A-Z, space, A-K-S, space, create which is the command to create an Azure Kubernetes Service Cluster or AKS for short.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=37)** After the create command, we'll need to specify a few parameters for our AKS cluster.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=43)** Type dash, dash, resource group for the resource group this cluster will belong to, followed by the name of the resource group you want to use.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=54)** I'll use dev.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=56)** Next, is the cluster name.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=59)** Type dash, dash name followed by the name you'd like to use.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=65)** Keep in mind that cluster names have a maximum of 63 characters and can use letters, numbers or dashes only.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=72)** A good naming convention for clusters usually includes the provider, the location or region it resides and then a meaningful name.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=82)** With these rules in mind, I'll name my cluster AKS, dash, west US two, dash, donuts.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=91)** At this point you have everything you need to create a cluster.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=95)** The resource group and name parameters are the only required parameters for creating one.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=101)** We're going to change ours slightly though.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=104)** Since this is going to be a dev cluster, where high availability isn't much of a priority as on a production cluster, we'll want to explicitly set the node count to one.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=115)** To do that, type dash, dash, node, dash, count followed by a one.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=123)** We do this as the default node count for AKS clusters is three.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=128)** If you do create a production-level cluster, leave the default.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=132)** This is recommended as your cluster will have fall-back nodes to rely on if one goes down.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=138)** Lastly we'll use the generate SSH keys parameter.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=142)** For that type dash, dash, generate, dash, SSH, dash, keys.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=152)** And this won't be followed by anything else.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=154)** As it's name implies, this parameter will generate SSH public and private key files we can use with our cluster.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=162)** Now, press enter.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=165)** It may take a few minutes for the cluster to be created.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=169)** If you do see a message like this, that's okay, go ahead and type Y for yes.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=175)** Go ahead and type Y for this too.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=179)** You should get a message like this that shows that you've added your SSH keys.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=183)** All that's left to do is to run the kube C-T-L, space, get, space, nodes command to verify that the single node is there.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=195)** And there it is.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/create-a-managed-kubernetes-cluster?u=76281980&t=196)** We've just created an Azure Kubernetes Service cluster with a single node.

### 9. 8. Securing your applications

#### Authentication options in Azure
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=0)** - Authentication and authorization are often confused for each other.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=6)** Authentication and authorization are related, but they're not the same thing.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=12)** It's worth exploring what each is and isn't so that you can properly secure your Azure applications.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=19)** Authentication answers the question are you the person you're claiming to be?
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=24)** When you need to use services online, like your bank's online portal, or your email, you're usually asked to provide proof that you are, in fact, who you say you are before you can proceed.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=36)** This is the authentication process and it's achieved in a few different ways.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=40)** The standard method is to confirm that a set of credentials, like a username and password, match a known user in the application's records.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=49)** If it does, an application can be mostly confident that the correct person is logging in by providing sufficient proof of their identity.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=57)** If it doesn't, an application must interpret that mismatch as insufficient proof of identity.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=64)** It's either the wrong person, or worse, a malicious person trying to login as someone else.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=72)** Another method is to use a third-party authentication service, to which this user check is now delegated.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=79)** In this method, the application relies on the third-party service to perform the identity check and to confirm the user against its own user records.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=89)** Once the service has confirmed or denied this person, the requesting application receives the determination as a result, then uses it to allow or deny the person logging in.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=102)** On the other hand, authorization answers the question are you allowed to perform this action?
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=110)** A great distinction to remember is that authorization usually occurs only after authentication has succeeded.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=118)** In other words, your bank's online portal, first and foremost, needs to make sure the person logging into your account is actually you.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=127)** Only then does it begin concerning itself with whether or not you're allowed to perform specific actions, like initiating a transfer, viewing your account balances, or setting withdrawal limits to your account.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=139)** It's easy to see how these two processes can become misconstrued for the other, as they work hand in hand.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=145)** Now that we know which is which, let's talk about the authentication options in Azure.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=152)** All Azure app services handle authentication and authorization within the same environment as your code.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=160)** This means that every request sent to your application goes through this security module and is authenticated by the identity provider you choose.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=169)** Identity providers are third-party services that manage user identities and authentication flows for the applications that use them.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=178)** Azure supports five identity providers by default, Azure Active Directory, a generic Microsoft account, or accounts from Facebook, Google, or Twitter.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=189)** When you enable one of these identity providers, you'll be able to use the provider's sign-in and point to authenticate users on your application.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=198)** You can also choose to integrate with your own custom identity provider.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=202)** However, it's not always recommended.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=205)** Security, especially when it comes to users' information, is quite difficult to implement correctly and robustly, which is why using one of the default providers is highly recommended.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=218)** Having a bug in this area of your application is something nobody wants.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=222)** So I urge you to offload that responsibility to one of the default identity providers.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=229)** Once your users are authenticated, a really neat feature of using these identity providers with Azure App Services is that you have a built-in token store at your disposal.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=239)** This store holds your users' tokens.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=243)** If you've ever had to manually manage, collect, and refresh users' tokens, you'll love the token store as much as me.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=251)** Instead of writing all that code to deal with tokens, we can just request them from the token store and even tell our app service to refresh them when we need to.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=259)** And if you're lucky enough to work with apps that don't use tokens, you can just disable the token store.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/azure-essential-training-for-developers/authentication-options-in-azure?u=76281980&t=266)** If you'd like to learn more about implementing authentication and authorization for your applications, I recommend checking out the Azure for Developers Security Best Practices Course for a deeper dive.
